<span id="freeTextContainer2530975742828720119">'Death may even be the greatest of all good things for a human being - no one knows, yet people fear it as if that knew for sure it's the greatest of bad things. And how is this kind of ignorance not reprehensible - thinking one knows what one doesn't?'<br/><br/>When charged with the crime of being faithless and an agent of corruption, Socrates defends himself in the court of Athens declaring that this accusation was the work of men who think themselves wise when they truly aren't and cannot bear to be to</span>
  <span id="freeText2530975742828720119" style="display:none">'Death may even be the greatest of all good things for a human being - no one knows, yet people fear it as if that knew for sure it's the greatest of bad things. And how is this kind of ignorance not reprehensible - thinking one knows what one doesn't?'<br /><br />When charged with the crime of being faithless and an agent of corruption, Socrates defends himself in the court of Athens declaring that this accusation was the work of men who think themselves wise when they truly aren't and cannot bear to be told that. Socrates wants to reason with the jury and not display sentiment or weakness and beg them. If he was to walk a free man after the trial, he wants the jury to be mindful that he would not thereby alter his ways.<br /><br />In the first section, Socrates puts forth his elaborate justifications. In the second section, he is found guilty and has to choose his mode of punishment. In the final section, he is issued a death penalty and delivers his last instructions to those who voted favorably.<br /><br />I am completely stumped by the sensible, honest, and principled self-defence dated 399 BC !</span>

<div id="review_1152781191" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1152781191" />
    <a title="Genni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4851711-genni"><img alt="Genni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518577245p2/4851711.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1152781191?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Genni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Genni" href="/user/show/4851711-genni">Genni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4851711-genni?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4851711-genni?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1152781191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6748413506215020542">I love reading Plato, but there is a gaping disadvantage in trying to write a review on something that has been "reviewed" almost constantly and critically for 2000+ years. Oh,well. I have to add my enthusiastic thoughts/rating to the stream.<br/><br/>While reading, I thought of Paul in Athens some 400 years after Socrates was condemned to death. The legacy of the Greek philosophers lived on and Acts declares that "the Athenians and the strangers visiting there used to spend their time in nothing other th</span>
  <span id="freeText6748413506215020542" style="display:none">I love reading Plato, but there is a gaping disadvantage in trying to write a review on something that has been "reviewed" almost constantly and critically for 2000+ years. Oh,well. I have to add my enthusiastic thoughts/rating to the stream.<br /><br />While reading, I thought of Paul in Athens some 400 years after Socrates was condemned to death. The legacy of the Greek philosophers lived on and Acts declares that "the Athenians and the strangers visiting there used to spend their time in nothing other than telling or hearing something new". How far they had come from condemning Socrates for introducing "new" gods and corrupting the young men of Athens! Of course, in his defense (literally), he was not teaching them anything new, but exposing what they did not know.<br /><br /><i>The Apology</i> is largely divided into three sections: his defense to the charges brought against him, his alternative penalty, and his response to the sentence of death. It is easy when reading his response to each decision to get lost in wondering when he is being serious, sincere, ironic, sarcastic, etc. But that aside, his defense contains some of the most insightful thoughts. The most well-known would probably be, "the unexamined life is not worth living" and "what I do not know I do not think I know". One of my favorites was, "For to fear death, gentlemen, is only to think you know what you don't know. No one knows whether death is really the greatest blessing a man can have, but they fear it is the greatest curse, as if they knew well." And of course, it contained a bit of the famous Socratic dialectic, almost as though Plato could not help himself.<br /><br />Being one of the most accessible of Plato's works, it is absolutely a must read; for historical and political reasons, but even more so for the personal insights it affords.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6748413506215020542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2445185118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2867079785120692520">Having started reading philosophy and its history this year, I already thought os Socrates as an utterly impressive philosopher; his questions were pretty darn legit despite what it brought him! Now, after reading his defence, and keeping aside the fact that Plato was his student and that this might not be the most unbiased writing, my admiration for this determined brave philosopher grows more. Good Read!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2445185118">

<div id="review_1373437510" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1373437510" />
    <a title="Marjolein" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5289598-marjolein"><img alt="Marjolein" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1303724282p2/5289598.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1373437510?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marjolein" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marjolein" href="/user/show/5289598-marjolein">Marjolein</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141397641" href="/book/show/24874318-socrates-defence">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5289598-marjolein?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5289598-marjolein?shelf=little-black-classics">little-black-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5289598-marjolein?shelf=review-pending">review-pending</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5289598-marjolein?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1373437510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8748059158581665016">I couldn't read this without hearing the song from Horrible Histories. Either way this was a very interesting read. I have read some Plato before for school, but never his more famous work on Socrates. Here he really tries to keep the style rather simple - for he is accused of bedazzling the audience with mighty words - while he makes a case for truth and knowledge. <br/><br/>~Little Black Classics #52~</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2038688104" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2038688104" />
    <a title="Ahmed Samir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27488361-ahmed-samir"><img alt="Ahmed Samir" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591582368p2/27488361.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2038688104?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmed Samir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmed Samir" href="/user/show/27488361-ahmed-samir">Ahmed Samir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2038688104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7987044992004880770">It's the apology which truly got me fascinated in philosophy. The book is quite demanding and I really do like the tone with which it is written. It doesn't necessarily discuss the most prominent philosophical conundrums Plato is famous for, but still an excellent read. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Z3wG3dnJepahKm5iv44DcgTmBNmuxypPAllkfe51BpSPtSm6dvh4dgT6h5eoYYEpal/BJ3JayF/7C+Qga+8ewA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OnQyfMUikkop+VZlBWVkg086tBMhhugN4BUvrb3b7U/SvR0bahOQqowpv5ASiubYIYNx7f0bCh0ZR6/wOEH1Gw==')})
  }

Su preocupación, su gran preocupación consistió, según ellos, en dar un fundamento lógico a la nueva ciencia natural, como él mismo lo intentó y lo hizo. Descartes es, de cierto, así un clásico en la historia de la filosofía como en clásico en la historia de la ciencia. Para otros, la intención acuciante e íntima de Descartes era de orden moral y religioso (L. Blanchet, por ejemplo) : apaciguar el conflicto entre revelación y razón, entre fe y saber. <span id="freeTextContainer3826480383338495898">(Greek: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4989143.______' title='Πλάτων' rel='nofollow noopener'>Πλάτων</a>) (Arabic: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1107355' rel='nofollow noopener'>أفلاطون</a>) (Alternate Spelling: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18647721.Platon' title='Platon' rel='nofollow noopener'>Platon</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17079660.Plat_n' title='Platón' rel='nofollow noopener'>Platón</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16372811.Platone' title='Platone' rel='nofollow noopener'>Platone</a>)<br/>Plato is a Classical Greek philosopher, mathematician, student of Socrates, writer of philosophical dialogues, and founder of the Academy in Athens, the first institution of higher learning in the Western world. Along with his mentor, Socrates, and his student, Aristotle, Plato helped to lay the foundations of Western p</span>
  <span id="freeText3826480383338495898" style="display:none">(Greek: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4989143.______" title="Πλάτων" rel="nofollow noopener">Πλάτων</a>) (Arabic: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1107355" rel="nofollow noopener">أفلاطون</a>) (Alternate Spelling: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18647721.Platon" title="Platon" rel="nofollow noopener">Platon</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17079660.Plat_n" title="Platón" rel="nofollow noopener">Platón</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16372811.Platone" title="Platone" rel="nofollow noopener">Platone</a>)<br />Plato is a Classical Greek philosopher, mathematician, student of Socrates, writer of philosophical dialogues, and founder of the Academy in Athens, the first institution of higher learning in the Western world. Along with his mentor, Socrates, and his student, Aristotle, Plato helped to lay the foundations of Western philosophy and science.<br /><br />Plato is one of the most important Western philosophers, exerting influence on virtually every figure in philosophy after him. His dialogue <i>The Republic</i> is known as the first comprehensive work on political philosophy. Plato also contributed foundationally to ethics, metaphysics, and epistemology. His student, Aristotle, is also an extremely influential philosopher and the tutor of Alexander the Great of Macedonia.</span>
